### Section: 7.1 Degrees of Freedom:

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamentals of digital communication, including the concepts of information, channel, and noise. We have also discussed the importance of modulation in digital communication systems, where the information is mapped onto a carrier signal for transmission. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of degrees of freedom and how they play a crucial role in digital communication.

#### 7.1a Definition of Degrees of Freedom

In physics, the degrees of freedom (DOF) of a mechanical system is the number of independent parameters that define its configuration or state. It is an important concept in the analysis of systems of bodies in mechanical engineering, structural engineering, aerospace engineering, robotics, and other fields.

In the context of digital communication, the degrees of freedom refer to the number of independent parameters that define the state of a signal. These parameters can include the amplitude, frequency, phase, and polarization of the signal. The number of degrees of freedom of a signal is directly related to its complexity and the amount of information it can carry.

For example, a simple sinusoidal signal has only one degree of freedom, as its state is defined by its amplitude and frequency. On the other hand, a more complex signal, such as a quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM) signal, has four degrees of freedom, as its state is defined by its amplitude, frequency, phase, and polarization.

The concept of degrees of freedom is closely related to the concept of signal space. In signal space, each point represents a unique signal, and the distance between two points represents the similarity between the corresponding signals. The number of degrees of freedom of a signal can be visualized as the number of dimensions in its signal space.

In digital communication, the number of degrees of freedom of a signal is a crucial factor in determining its ability to carry information. A signal with more degrees of freedom can carry more information, but it also requires more complex modulation techniques to transmit. Therefore, understanding the concept of degrees of freedom is essential in designing efficient and reliable digital communication systems.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of modulation techniques and how they utilize the degrees of freedom of a signal to transmit information. We will also discuss the concept of constellation diagrams, which are graphical representations of signal space, and how they are used in modulation schemes. By the end of this section, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of degrees of freedom and how they are used in digital communication systems.


#### 7.1b Importance of Degrees of Freedom

The concept of degrees of freedom is crucial in understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems. As mentioned earlier, the number of degrees of freedom of a signal is directly related to its complexity and the amount of information it can carry. In this subsection, we will explore the importance of degrees of freedom in digital communication and how it affects the performance of communication systems.

One of the key factors in determining the performance of a communication system is the amount of information that can be transmitted over a given bandwidth. This is known as the channel capacity, and it is directly related to the number of degrees of freedom of the signal. The more degrees of freedom a signal has, the higher its channel capacity, and the more information it can carry.

In digital communication, the number of degrees of freedom of a signal is also closely related to the modulation scheme used. Modulation is the process of mapping information onto a carrier signal for transmission. Different modulation schemes use different numbers of degrees of freedom to transmit information. For example, amplitude modulation (AM) only uses one degree of freedom, while quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM) uses four degrees of freedom. This means that QAM can transmit more information than AM over the same bandwidth.

Another important aspect of degrees of freedom is its relationship with the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR). The SNR is the ratio of the signal power to the noise power, and it is a crucial factor in determining the quality of a received signal. The more degrees of freedom a signal has, the more sensitive it is to changes in the SNR. This means that a signal with more degrees of freedom will experience a larger decrease in quality when the SNR decreases.

In addition to its impact on channel capacity and SNR, the number of degrees of freedom also affects the robustness of a communication system. A system with more degrees of freedom is more resilient to noise and interference, as it has more parameters to compensate for changes in the channel. This is especially important in wireless communication, where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably.

In conclusion, the concept of degrees of freedom is crucial in understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems. It affects the channel capacity, SNR, and robustness of a system, and is closely related to the modulation scheme used. In the next section, we will explore the different types of modulation schemes and how they utilize the degrees of freedom of a signal.


#### 7.1c Degrees of Freedom in Digital Communication

In digital communication, the concept of degrees of freedom plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of signals. As mentioned earlier, the number of degrees of freedom of a signal is directly related to its complexity and the amount of information it can carry. In this subsection, we will explore the concept of degrees of freedom in digital communication and how it affects the performance of communication systems.

One of the key factors in determining the performance of a communication system is the amount of information that can be transmitted over a given bandwidth. This is known as the channel capacity, and it is directly related to the number of degrees of freedom of the signal. The more degrees of freedom a signal has, the higher its channel capacity, and the more information it can carry.

In digital communication, the number of degrees of freedom of a signal is also closely related to the modulation scheme used. Modulation is the process of mapping information onto a carrier signal for transmission. Different modulation schemes use different numbers of degrees of freedom to transmit information. For example, amplitude modulation (AM) only uses one degree of freedom, while quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM) uses four degrees of freedom. This means that QAM can transmit more information than AM over the same bandwidth.

Another important aspect of degrees of freedom is its relationship with the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR). The SNR is the ratio of the signal power to the noise power, and it is a crucial factor in determining the quality of a received signal. The more degrees of freedom a signal has, the more sensitive it is to changes in the SNR. This means that a signal with more degrees of freedom will experience a larger decrease in quality when the SNR decreases.

In addition to its impact on channel capacity and SNR, the number of degrees of freedom also affects the robustness of a communication system. A system with more degrees of freedom is more resilient to noise and interference, as it has more parameters to compensate for changes in the channel. This is especially important in wireless communication, where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably.

In digital communication, the concept of degrees of freedom is closely related to the concept of signal space. In signal space, each point represents a unique signal, and the number of dimensions in the space is equal to the number of degrees of freedom of the signal. This means that a signal with more degrees of freedom will occupy a larger space in signal space, allowing for more information to be transmitted.

Furthermore, the concept of degrees of freedom is also closely related to the concept of modulation. Modulation is the process of mapping information onto a carrier signal, and the number of degrees of freedom of the signal determines the complexity of the modulation scheme. For example, AM only uses one degree of freedom, while QAM uses four degrees of freedom. This means that QAM can transmit more information than AM over the same bandwidth.

In conclusion, the concept of degrees of freedom is crucial in understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems. It affects the channel capacity, SNR, robustness, and modulation schemes of a communication system. By understanding the concept of degrees of freedom, we can design more efficient and robust communication systems for the digital age.





#### 7.1b Importance of Degrees of Freedom

The number of degrees of freedom of a signal is a critical factor in digital communication systems. It directly influences the amount of information that can be transmitted over a given bandwidth. The more degrees of freedom a signal has, the more information it can carry.

In the context of modulation, the degrees of freedom of a signal determine the number of different signals that can be transmitted simultaneously over the same bandwidth. For example, in a binary modulation scheme, each signal can have two different states (e.g., high and low amplitude), and thus two signals can be transmitted simultaneously. In a QAM modulation scheme, each signal can have four different states (e.g., high and low amplitude, and 0 and 90 degree phase shift), and thus four signals can be transmitted simultaneously.

Furthermore, the degrees of freedom of a signal also influence its robustness against noise. A signal with more degrees of freedom is less susceptible to noise, as it can spread its information over a larger signal space. This makes it more likely that the signal will be able to successfully decode the transmitted information, even in the presence of noise.

In summary, the number of degrees of freedom of a signal is a fundamental concept in digital communication. It determines the amount of information that can be transmitted, the number of signals that can be transmitted simultaneously, and the robustness of the signal against noise. Understanding and manipulating the degrees of freedom of signals is a key skill in the design and analysis of digital communication systems.

#### 7.1c Degrees of Freedom in Digital Communication

In digital communication, the degrees of freedom of a signal are often manipulated to optimize the transmission of information. This is achieved through various modulation techniques, which map the information onto the degrees of freedom of the signal.

One such technique is the use of multiple degrees of freedom systems and mode shapes in vibration analysis. As we have seen in the previous section, a system with more masses that move in more than one direction adds degrees of freedom. This allows for more complex signals to be transmitted, carrying more information.

The equations of motion for a multiple degrees of freedom system can be written as:

$$
\begin{bmatrix}m_1 & 0\\ 0 & m_2\end{bmatrix}\begin{Bmatrix}\ddot{x_1}\\ \ddot{x_2} \end{Bmatrix} + \begin{bmatrix} c_1+c_2 & -c_2\\ -c_2 & c_2+c_3\end{bmatrix}\begin{Bmatrix}\dot{x_1}\\ \dot{x_2}\end{Bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}k_1+k_2 & -k_2\\ -k_2 & k_2+k_3\end{bmatrix}\begin{Bmatrix} x_1\\ x_2\end{Bmatrix}=\begin{Bmatrix} f_1\\ f_2\end{Bmatrix}.
$$

These equations represent the dynamics of the system, where the matrices $M$, $C$, and $K$ are symmetric matrices referred to as the mass, damping, and stiffness matrices, respectively. The number of degrees of freedom of the system is determined by the size of these matrices, $N$.

In the case of free vibration, where there is no damping and no applied forces, the solution to these equations involves assuming the system to be in a state of harmonic motion. This allows for the determination of the natural frequencies and mode shapes of the system, which are crucial in understanding the behavior of the system under different conditions.

The natural frequencies of the system are determined by the eigenvalues of the matrix $K$, while the mode shapes are determined by the eigenvectors of the matrix $K$. The natural frequencies represent the resonant frequencies of the system, at which the system oscillates with maximum amplitude. The mode shapes represent the patterns of oscillation of the system at these frequencies.

In digital communication, these concepts are used to design modulation schemes that can efficiently transmit information over a given bandwidth. By manipulating the degrees of freedom of the signal, it is possible to create complex signals that can carry large amounts of information. This is achieved through the use of modulation techniques that map the information onto the natural frequencies and mode shapes of the system.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into these modulation techniques and explore how they are used in digital communication systems.




#### 7.1c Degrees of Freedom in Digital Communication

In digital communication, the degrees of freedom of a signal are often manipulated to optimize the transmission of information. This is achieved through various modulation techniques, which map the information onto the degrees of freedom of the signal.

One such technique is the use of multiple degrees of freedom (DOF) in digital communication. This technique allows for the transmission of multiple signals simultaneously over the same bandwidth, thereby increasing the overall capacity of the communication system.

The concept of DOF in digital communication can be understood in the context of the IEEE 802.11ah standard. This standard, which is used for wireless communication, defines the concept of DOF as the number of independent signals that can be transmitted simultaneously over the same bandwidth. In other words, it is the number of different signals that can be mapped onto the same set of frequencies.

The IEEE 802.11ah standard also defines the concept of DOF in terms of the classical capacity of a communication system. The classical capacity, denoted by C, is the maximum amount of classical information that can be transmitted by non-entangled encodings over parallel channel uses. It is calculated as:

$$
C = \max_{p\in[0,1]} \Big\{ H_2 \left(\eta \, p \right)- H_2 \left(\frac{1 + \sqrt{1- 4 \,\eta\,(1-\eta) \,p ^2}}{2} \right) \Big\}
$$

where $\eta$ is the average value of the population and coherence term, and $p$ is the average value of the population term.

The concept of DOF is also closely related to the concept of signal space. In digital communication, the signal space is the space in which the signals are transmitted. Each point in this space represents a different signal. By manipulating the DOF, we can control the number of points in the signal space, and thus the number of signals that can be transmitted simultaneously.

In conclusion, the concept of DOF plays a crucial role in digital communication. It allows us to optimize the transmission of information by mapping the information onto the degrees of freedom of the signal. By understanding and manipulating the DOF, we can design more efficient and robust digital communication systems.




#### 7.2a Definition of Orthonormal Expansions

Orthonormal expansions are a fundamental concept in digital communication, particularly in the context of signal space and modulation. They are a set of functions that are orthogonal to each other and have a unit norm. In other words, the inner product of any two orthonormal functions is zero, and the norm of each function is 1.

Mathematically, an orthonormal set of functions $\{f_n(t)\}$ is defined as:

$$
\langle f_n(t), f_m(t) \rangle = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} f_n(t) f_m^*(t) dt = \delta_{nm}
$$

where $\delta_{nm}$ is the Kronecker delta, which is 1 if $n = m$ and 0 otherwise.

Orthonormal expansions are particularly useful in digital communication because they allow us to represent any signal as a sum of orthonormal functions. This is known as the Parseval theorem, which states that the total energy of a signal is preserved when it is represented as a sum of orthonormal functions.

In the context of digital communication, orthonormal expansions are often used to represent signals in the signal space. Each orthonormal function represents a point in the signal space, and the coefficients of the expansion represent the amplitude of the signal at each point. By manipulating these coefficients, we can control the shape of the signal in the signal space, and thus the characteristics of the transmitted signal.

In the next section, we will explore how orthonormal expansions are used in the context of modulation, and how they can be used to optimize the transmission of information in digital communication systems.

#### 7.2b Properties of Orthonormal Expansions

Orthonormal expansions have several important properties that make them a powerful tool in digital communication. These properties are derived from the defining property of orthonormal functions, namely that the inner product of any two orthonormal functions is zero.

1. **Parseval Theorem**: As mentioned in the previous section, the Parseval theorem states that the total energy of a signal is preserved when it is represented as a sum of orthonormal functions. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} |f(t)|^2 dt = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} |a_n|^2
$$

where $f(t)$ is the original signal, $a_n$ are the coefficients of the orthonormal expansion, and $|a_n|^2$ are the squared coefficients.

2. **Orthogonality**: The orthogonality property of orthonormal functions ensures that the coefficients of the expansion are independent of each other. This means that changing the coefficient of one function does not affect the coefficients of the other functions.

3. **Completeness**: The completeness property of orthonormal expansions states that any function can be approximated arbitrarily closely by a sum of orthonormal functions. This is a consequence of the Parseval theorem, which ensures that the total energy of the signal is preserved in the expansion.

4. **Energy Concentration**: The energy concentration property of orthonormal expansions states that the energy of a signal is concentrated at the points where the orthonormal functions have large coefficients. This property is particularly useful in digital communication, where we often want to concentrate the energy of the signal at specific points in the signal space.

5. **Frequency Localization**: The frequency localization property of orthonormal expansions states that the frequency content of a signal is localized at the points where the orthonormal functions have large coefficients. This property is particularly useful in modulation, where we often want to localize the frequency content of the signal at specific points in the frequency space.

In the next section, we will explore how these properties of orthonormal expansions are used in the context of modulation, and how they can be used to optimize the transmission of information in digital communication systems.

#### 7.2c Orthonormal Expansions in Digital Communication

In digital communication, orthonormal expansions play a crucial role in the representation and transmission of signals. The properties of orthonormal expansions, as discussed in the previous section, are particularly useful in this context.

1. **Parseval Theorem in Digital Communication**: In digital communication, the Parseval theorem is used to ensure that the total energy of a signal is preserved when it is represented as a sum of orthonormal functions. This is particularly important in digital communication systems, where the signal is often transmitted over a noisy channel. The Parseval theorem ensures that the total energy of the signal is not affected by the noise, which allows us to recover the original signal at the receiver.

2. **Orthogonality in Digital Communication**: The orthogonality property of orthonormal functions is used to simplify the representation of signals in digital communication. By ensuring that the coefficients of the expansion are independent of each other, we can represent complex signals in a compact and efficient manner. This is particularly useful in digital communication systems, where the signals often have a high dimensionality.

3. **Completeness in Digital Communication**: The completeness property of orthonormal expansions is used to approximate signals in digital communication. By approximating a signal with a sum of orthonormal functions, we can represent the signal with a finite number of coefficients. This is particularly useful in digital communication systems, where the signals often have a high dimensionality and need to be represented with a finite number of coefficients.

4. **Energy Concentration in Digital Communication**: The energy concentration property of orthonormal expansions is used to focus the energy of a signal in digital communication. By concentrating the energy of the signal at specific points in the signal space, we can transmit the signal more efficiently and reliably. This is particularly useful in digital communication systems, where the signals often need to be transmitted over a noisy channel.

5. **Frequency Localization in Digital Communication**: The frequency localization property of orthonormal expansions is used to localize the frequency content of a signal in digital communication. By localizing the frequency content of the signal at specific points in the frequency space, we can transmit the signal more efficiently and reliably. This is particularly useful in digital communication systems, where the signals often need to be transmitted over a noisy channel.

In the next section, we will explore how these properties of orthonormal expansions are used in the context of modulation, and how they can be used to optimize the transmission of information in digital communication systems.




#### 7.2b Importance of Orthonormal Expansions

Orthonormal expansions are a fundamental concept in digital communication, particularly in the context of signal space and modulation. They are a set of functions that are orthogonal to each other and have a unit norm. In other words, the inner product of any two orthonormal functions is zero, and the norm of each function is 1.

Mathematically, an orthonormal set of functions $\{f_n(t)\}$ is defined as:

$$
\langle f_n(t), f_m(t) \rangle = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} f_n(t) f_m^*(t) dt = \delta_{nm}
$$

where $\delta_{nm}$ is the Kronecker delta, which is 1 if $n = m$ and 0 otherwise.

Orthonormal expansions are particularly useful in digital communication because they allow us to represent any signal as a sum of orthonormal functions. This is known as the Parseval theorem, which states that the total energy of a signal is preserved when it is represented as a sum of orthonormal functions.

In the context of digital communication, orthonormal expansions are often used to represent signals in the signal space. Each orthonormal function represents a point in the signal space, and the coefficients of the expansion represent the amplitude of the signal at each point. By manipulating these coefficients, we can control the shape of the signal in the signal space, and thus the characteristics of the transmitted signal.

The importance of orthonormal expansions in digital communication cannot be overstated. They provide a powerful tool for signal representation and manipulation, and are fundamental to many of the techniques used in digital communication systems. In the following sections, we will explore some of these techniques in more detail.

#### 7.2c Orthonormal Expansions in Digital Communication

In digital communication, orthonormal expansions play a crucial role in the representation and manipulation of signals. The ability to represent any signal as a sum of orthonormal functions, as stated by the Parseval theorem, allows us to control the shape of the signal in the signal space. This is particularly important in digital communication, where we often need to transmit signals over noisy channels.

One of the key applications of orthonormal expansions in digital communication is in the design of modulation schemes. Modulation is the process of converting a digital signal into an analog signal for transmission over a communication channel. The design of a modulation scheme involves choosing a set of orthonormal functions that will be used to represent the digital signal. The choice of these functions can significantly impact the performance of the modulation scheme, particularly in the presence of noise.

For example, consider a binary phase shift keying (BPSK) modulation scheme. In this scheme, the digital signal is represented as a sequence of +1s and -1s. The orthonormal functions used in this scheme are typically sinusoids of different frequencies. The digital signal is then transmitted by varying the amplitude of these sinusoids.

The performance of the BPSK modulation scheme can be analyzed using the concept of signal-to-noise ratio (SNR). The SNR is defined as the ratio of the power of the signal to the power of the noise. In the context of orthonormal expansions, the SNR can be expressed as:

$$
\text{SNR} = \frac{\sum_{n=1}^{N} |a_n|^2}{\sum_{n=1}^{N} |b_n|^2}
$$

where $a_n$ and $b_n$ are the coefficients of the orthonormal functions in the signal and noise, respectively, and $N$ is the number of orthonormal functions.

The SNR is a measure of the quality of the transmitted signal. A high SNR indicates that the signal is less corrupted by noise, while a low SNR indicates that the signal is more corrupted by noise. By choosing the orthonormal functions carefully, we can design a modulation scheme that achieves a high SNR, even in the presence of noise.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of orthonormal expansions in digital communication, including the use of orthonormal expansions in the design of modulation schemes.




#### 7.2c Orthonormal Expansions in Digital Communication

In digital communication, orthonormal expansions are used to represent signals in a way that preserves the total energy of the signal. This is particularly useful in the context of signal space and modulation, where signals are often represented as points in a multi-dimensional space.

Consider a signal $x(t)$ that can be represented as a sum of orthonormal functions $\{f_n(t)\}$:

$$
x(t) = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} a_n f_n(t)
$$

where $a_n$ are the coefficients of the expansion. The Parseval theorem states that the total energy of the signal is preserved under this representation:

$$
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} |x(t)|^2 dt = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} |a_n|^2
$$

In digital communication, orthonormal expansions are often used to represent signals in the signal space. Each orthonormal function represents a point in the signal space, and the coefficients of the expansion represent the amplitude of the signal at each point. By manipulating these coefficients, we can control the shape of the signal in the signal space, and thus the characteristics of the transmitted signal.

For example, consider a signal $x(t)$ that is represented in the signal space as a point $P$. The signal $x(t)$ can be reconstructed from the point $P$ by summing the products of the coefficients $a_n$ and the orthonormal functions $f_n(t)$:

$$
x(t) = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} a_n f_n(t)
$$

By manipulating the coefficients $a_n$, we can move the point $P$ around in the signal space, and thus change the characteristics of the signal $x(t)$.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of how orthonormal expansions are used in digital communication, including their role in modulation techniques.




#### 7.3a Definition of Signal Space

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of signal space and its importance in digital communication. In this section, we will delve deeper into the definition of signal space and its implications in the context of digital communication.

Signal space is a mathematical concept that represents the set of all possible signals. Each point in the signal space corresponds to a unique signal, and the distance between two points represents the difference between the corresponding signals. The signal space is typically represented as a multi-dimensional space, with each dimension representing a different aspect of the signal.

In the context of digital communication, the signal space is often represented as a high-dimensional space, with each dimension representing a different sample of the signal. For example, a one-dimensional signal can be represented as a point in a one-dimensional signal space, while a two-dimensional signal can be represented as a point in a two-dimensional signal space.

The concept of signal space is closely related to the concept of orthonormal expansions. In fact, the signal space can be viewed as the set of all possible orthonormal expansions of a signal. Each point in the signal space corresponds to a unique orthonormal expansion, and the distance between two points represents the difference between the corresponding expansions.

The signal space is a fundamental concept in digital communication, as it provides a mathematical framework for understanding and manipulating signals. By representing signals as points in the signal space, we can easily visualize the effects of different operations on the signals. For example, we can visualize the effects of filtering a signal by representing the filter as a line in the signal space.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of modulation, which is a key technique for transmitting signals in digital communication systems. We will see how modulation can be understood in terms of signal space, and how it can be used to transmit signals efficiently and reliably.

#### 7.3b Properties of Signal Space

The properties of signal space are crucial to understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems. These properties are derived from the fundamental principles of signal space and provide a mathematical framework for analyzing and manipulating signals.

##### Orthogonality

One of the key properties of signal space is orthogonality. Two signals are said to be orthogonal if their inner product is equal to zero. In the context of signal space, this means that two signals represented as points in the space are orthogonal if the distance between them is equal to the maximum possible distance. This property is fundamental to the concept of orthonormal expansions, as it allows us to represent signals as linear combinations of orthonormal functions.

##### Linearity

Another important property of signal space is linearity. This property states that the sum of two signals is equal to the sum of their individual components. In the context of signal space, this means that the sum of two points in the space is equal to the sum of their individual components. This property is crucial for understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems, as it allows us to manipulate signals by adding or subtracting their individual components.

##### Continuity

The property of continuity is also important in signal space. This property states that small changes in the input result in small changes in the output. In the context of signal space, this means that small changes in the representation of a signal result in small changes in the signal itself. This property is crucial for understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems, as it allows us to predict the effects of small changes in the representation of a signal.

##### Completeness

The property of completeness is also important in signal space. This property states that any signal can be represented as a linear combination of orthonormal functions. In the context of signal space, this means that any point in the space can be represented as a linear combination of the points representing the orthonormal functions. This property is crucial for understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems, as it allows us to represent any signal as a point in the signal space.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of modulation, which is a key technique for transmitting signals in digital communication systems. We will see how these properties of signal space are used in the context of modulation, and how they provide a mathematical framework for understanding and manipulating signals.

#### 7.3c Signal Space in Digital Communication

In the context of digital communication, signal space plays a crucial role in the transmission and reception of signals. The properties of signal space, as discussed in the previous section, are fundamental to understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems.

##### Signal Representation

In digital communication, signals are typically represented as points in the signal space. Each point in the space corresponds to a unique signal, and the distance between two points represents the difference between the corresponding signals. This representation allows us to visualize the effects of different operations on the signals, such as filtering or modulation.

##### Modulation

Modulation is a key technique used in digital communication systems. It involves the process of modifying a carrier signal with a message signal to transmit information. In the context of signal space, modulation can be understood as a mapping from the message signal space to the carrier signal space. This mapping is typically linear, and it is represented as a point in the signal space. The properties of orthogonality and linearity are crucial for understanding the behavior of modulated signals.

##### Demodulation

Demodulation is the inverse process of modulation. It involves the process of extracting the message signal from the modulated carrier signal. In the context of signal space, demodulation can be understood as a mapping from the carrier signal space to the message signal space. This mapping is also typically linear, and it is represented as a point in the signal space. The properties of orthogonality and linearity are crucial for understanding the behavior of demodulated signals.

##### Noise and Distortion

In digital communication systems, signals are often corrupted by noise and distortion. These effects can be represented in the signal space as perturbations of the signal points. The property of continuity is crucial for predicting the effects of small changes in the signal representation due to noise and distortion.

##### Channel Capacity

The concept of channel capacity is also important in digital communication. It represents the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a communication channel. In the context of signal space, channel capacity can be understood as the maximum number of points that can be represented in the signal space. The property of completeness is crucial for understanding the behavior of signals in digital communication systems, as it allows us to represent any signal as a point in the signal space.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of modulation and demodulation, and explore how they are used in digital communication systems.




#### 7.3b Importance of Signal Space

The concept of signal space is crucial in digital communication, as it provides a mathematical framework for understanding and manipulating signals. By representing signals as points in the signal space, we can easily visualize the effects of different operations on the signals. This allows us to design and analyze digital communication systems more effectively.

One of the key applications of signal space is in the design of modulation schemes. Modulation is a technique used to transmit information over a communication channel. It involves converting the information signal into a form that can be transmitted over the channel, and then decoding it at the receiver. The design of a modulation scheme often involves finding the optimal mapping between the information signal and the transmitted signal. This can be visualized as finding the optimal path in the signal space.

For example, consider a simple binary modulation scheme, where the information signal is a binary sequence, and the transmitted signal is a sequence of pulses. The signal space in this case is a two-dimensional space, with each dimension representing a different pulse. The optimal path in this space is the one that minimizes the error probability at the receiver.

Another important application of signal space is in the design of filters. Filters are used to remove unwanted components from a signal. In the context of digital communication, filters are often used to remove noise from the transmitted signal. The design of a filter often involves finding the optimal filter coefficients that minimize the error between the desired signal and the filtered signal. This can be visualized as finding the optimal point in the signal space.

In conclusion, the concept of signal space is a fundamental concept in digital communication. It provides a mathematical framework for understanding and manipulating signals, and it has numerous applications in the design of digital communication systems. By understanding the principles of signal space and modulation, we can design more efficient and reliable digital communication systems.

#### 7.3c Signal Space in Digital Communication

In digital communication, the concept of signal space is particularly important due to the nature of digital signals. Digital signals are discrete-time signals, meaning they are sampled at specific time intervals. This discrete nature allows us to represent the signal as a vector in a multi-dimensional space, where each dimension corresponds to a sample of the signal.

The signal space in digital communication is often represented as a high-dimensional space, with each dimension representing a different sample of the signal. This is because digital signals can have a large number of samples, especially in high-speed communication systems. For example, a digital signal with a sampling rate of 1 GHz would have a signal space with 1 billion dimensions.

The concept of signal space is particularly useful in the design of digital communication systems. By representing the signal as a point in the signal space, we can easily visualize the effects of different operations on the signal. This allows us to design and analyze digital communication systems more effectively.

This can be visualized as finding the optimal path in the signal space.

The signal space in this case is a two-dimensional space, with each dimension representing a different pulse. The optimal path in this space is the one that minimizes the error probability at the receiver.

#### 7.3c Signal Space in Digital Communication

In digital communication, the concept of signal space is particularly important due to the nature of digital signals. Digital signals are discrete-time signals, meaning they are sampled at specific time intervals. This discrete nature allows us to represent the signal as a point in a multi-dimensional space, where each dimension corresponds to a sample of the signal.

The signal space in digital communication is often visualized as a constellation diagram, where each point represents a possible value of the signal. For example, in a binary phase shift keying (BPSK) system, the signal space is a two-dimensional space, with each point representing a possible phase shift of the carrier signal. The optimal path in this space is the one that minimizes the error probability at the receiver.

The concept of signal space is also crucial in the design of modulation schemes. Modulation is a technique used to transmit information over a communication channel. It involves converting the information signal into a form that can be transmitted over the channel, and then decoding it at the receiver. The design of a modulation scheme often involves finding the optimal mapping between the information signal and the transmitted signal. This can be visualized as finding the optimal path in the signal space.

For example, consider a simple binary modulation scheme, where the information signal is a binary sequence, and the transmitted signal is a sequence of pulses. The signal space in this case is a two-dimensional space, with each dimension representing a different pulse. The optimal path in this space is the one that minimizes the error probability at the receiver.

Another important application of signal space is in the design of filters. Filters are used to remove unwanted components from a signal. In the context of digital communication, filters are often used to remove noise from the transmitted signal. The design of a filter often involves finding the optimal filter coefficients that minimize the error between the desired signal and the filtered signal. This can be visualized as finding the optimal point in the signal space.

In conclusion, the concept of signal space is a fundamental concept in digital communication. It provides a mathematical framework for understanding and manipulating signals, and it has numerous applications in the design of digital communication systems.




#### 7.4a Definition of Modulation

Modulation is a fundamental concept in digital communication. It is the process of varying one or more properties of a carrier signal with a separate signal, known as the modulation signal, which typically carries information to be transmitted. The modulation signal can be an audio signal representing sound from a microphone, a video signal representing moving images from a video camera, or a digital signal representing a sequence of binary digits, a bitstream from a computer.

The carrier signal, which is usually a high-frequency wave, is modulated to carry the information contained in the modulation signal. This modulation process is necessary because it is impractical to transmit signals with low frequencies. In radio communication, for example, the modulated carrier is transmitted through space as a radio wave to a radio receiver.

Another purpose of modulation is to transmit multiple channels of information through a single communication medium, using frequency-division multiplexing (FDM). For instance, in cable television, which uses FDM, many carrier signals, each modulated with a different television channel, are transported through a single cable to customers. Since each carrier occupies a different frequency, the channels do not interfere with each other. At the destination end, the carrier signal is demodulated to extract the information bearing modulation signal.

A modulator is a device or circuit that performs modulation. A demodulator, sometimes referred to as a detector, is a circuit that performs demodulation, the inverse of modulation. A modem (from modulator–demodulator), used in bidirectional communication, can perform both operations.

The lower frequency band occupied by the modulation signal is called the "baseband", while the higher frequency band occupied by the modulated carrier is called the "passband". The passband is typically much wider than the baseband, allowing for efficient use of the available bandwidth.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the different types of modulation techniques and their applications in digital communication.

#### 7.4b Types of Modulation

There are several types of modulation techniques used in digital communication. These include Amplitude Modulation (AM), Frequency Modulation (FM), Phase Modulation (PM), and Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM). Each of these modulation techniques has its own unique characteristics and applications.

##### Amplitude Modulation (AM)

Amplitude Modulation (AM) is a type of modulation where the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the modulation signal. The amplitude of the carrier signal is directly proportional to the amplitude of the modulation signal. Mathematically, this can be represented as:

$$
s(t) = (1 + m(t)) \cdot A \cdot \cos(2\pi f_c t)
$$

where $s(t)$ is the modulated signal, $m(t)$ is the modulation signal, $A$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, and $f_c$ is the carrier frequency.

AM is widely used in commercial radio broadcasting. The modulation signal, which is the audio signal, varies the amplitude of the carrier signal, which is the radio frequency signal. The modulated signal is then transmitted through the air to the radio receivers.

##### Frequency Modulation (FM)

Frequency Modulation (FM) is a type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the modulation signal. The frequency of the carrier signal is directly proportional to the amplitude of the modulation signal. This can be represented as:

$$
s(t) = A \cdot \cos(2\pi f_c t + \Delta \phi(t))
$$

where $s(t)$ is the modulated signal, $A$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $\Delta \phi(t)$ is the phase deviation caused by the modulation signal.

FM is used in commercial radio broadcasting and in radar systems. The modulation signal, which is the audio signal or the radar pulse, varies the frequency of the carrier signal, which is the radio frequency signal or the radar wave. The modulated signal is then transmitted through the air to the radio receivers or the radar receivers.

##### Phase Modulation (PM)

Phase Modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the modulation signal. The phase of the carrier signal is directly proportional to the amplitude of the modulation signal. This can be represented as:

$$
s(t) = A \cdot \cos(2\pi f_c t + k_p m(t))
$$

where $s(t)$ is the modulated signal, $A$ is the amplitude of the carrier signal, $f_c$ is the carrier frequency, and $k_p$ is the phase sensitivity constant.

PM is used in digital communication systems, particularly in Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) systems. The modulation signal, which is the digital data stream, varies the phase of the carrier signal, which is the radio frequency signal. The modulated signal is then transmitted through the air to the radio receivers.

##### Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM)

Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) is a type of modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied in accordance with the modulation signal. This allows for the simultaneous transmission of multiple signals, making it particularly useful in digital communication systems. The modulation signal, which is the digital data stream, varies both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal, which is the radio frequency signal. The modulated signal is then transmitted through the air to the radio receivers.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the principles of these modulation techniques and their applications in digital communication systems.

#### 7.4c Modulation in Digital Communication

Modulation plays a crucial role in digital communication systems. It is the process of varying one or more properties of a carrier signal with a separate signal, known as the modulation signal, which typically carries information to be transmitted. The modulation signal can be an audio signal representing sound from a microphone, a video signal representing moving images from a video camera, or a digital signal representing a sequence of binary digits, a bitstream from a computer.

In digital communication, the modulation signal is often a digital signal, which is a sequence of binary digits (bits). The digital signal is used to vary the properties of the carrier signal, such as its amplitude, frequency, or phase. This process is known as digital modulation.

Digital modulation is used in a variety of communication systems, including wireless communication, satellite communication, and fiber optic communication. It is particularly useful in these systems because it allows for the efficient transmission of large amounts of data.

One of the key advantages of digital modulation is its ability to transmit multiple signals simultaneously. This is achieved through a technique known as frequency-division multiplexing (FDM), where multiple signals are transmitted over a single communication channel by assigning each signal a different frequency band.

Another important aspect of digital modulation is error correction. In digital communication, it is crucial to ensure that the transmitted data is received accurately. This is achieved through error correction codes, which are mathematical algorithms that can detect and correct errors in the transmitted data.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the principles of digital modulation and explore some of the most common types of digital modulation techniques, including amplitude-shift keying (ASK), frequency-shift keying (FSK), and phase-shift keying (PSK).




#### 7.4b Importance of Modulation

Modulation plays a crucial role in digital communication systems. It is the process that allows the efficient transmission of information over long distances. Without modulation, it would be impossible to transmit signals over large distances due to signal degradation and interference.

One of the primary reasons for modulation is to increase the bandwidth of a signal. As mentioned earlier, the bandwidth of a signal is the range of frequencies that it occupies. In digital communication, the bandwidth is limited, and there is a need to transmit multiple signals simultaneously. Modulation allows us to increase the bandwidth of a signal by spreading it over a wider range of frequencies. This is achieved by varying one or more properties of a carrier signal with the modulation signal.

Another important aspect of modulation is its ability to combat interference. In a communication system, signals can be subjected to various types of interference, such as noise, distortion, and fading. These interferences can degrade the quality of the received signal. However, modulation techniques, such as frequency modulation (FM) and amplitude modulation (AM), are designed to minimize the effects of these interferences. For instance, in FM, the information is encoded in the frequency of the carrier signal, which is less susceptible to noise and distortion compared to the amplitude of the signal.

Modulation also plays a crucial role in frequency-division multiplexing (FDM), a technique used in communication systems to transmit multiple signals simultaneously over a single communication channel. Each signal is assigned a different frequency band, and these bands are then combined to form a composite signal. Modulation is used to convert each signal into a form suitable for transmission over the assigned frequency band.

In conclusion, modulation is a fundamental concept in digital communication. It allows us to increase the bandwidth of a signal, combat interference, and transmit multiple signals simultaneously. Understanding modulation is essential for anyone studying digital communication systems.

#### 7.4c Modulation Techniques

Modulation techniques are the methods used to convert a modulating signal into a modulated carrier signal. These techniques can be broadly classified into two categories: analog modulation and digital modulation. Analog modulation techniques include amplitude modulation (AM), frequency modulation (FM), and phase modulation (PM). Digital modulation techniques include on-off keying (OOK), amplitude-shift keying (ASK), and frequency-shift keying (FSK).

##### Analog Modulation Techniques

Analog modulation techniques are used in traditional radio and television broadcasting systems. In these techniques, the modulating signal is a continuous signal, and the modulated carrier signal is also a continuous signal.

###### Amplitude Modulation (AM)

In amplitude modulation, the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied in proportion to the amplitude of the modulating signal. The resulting modulated signal has a bandwidth that is twice the bandwidth of the modulating signal. This is due to the fact that the modulated signal contains both the original modulating signal and its upper sideband.

###### Frequency Modulation (FM)

In frequency modulation, the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in proportion to the amplitude of the modulating signal. The resulting modulated signal has a bandwidth that is greater than the bandwidth of the modulating signal. This is because the modulated signal contains both the original modulating signal and its upper and lower sidebands.

###### Phase Modulation (PM)

In phase modulation, the phase of the carrier signal is varied in proportion to the amplitude of the modulating signal. The resulting modulated signal has a bandwidth that is twice the bandwidth of the modulating signal. This is due to the fact that the modulated signal contains both the original modulating signal and its upper sideband.

##### Digital Modulation Techniques

Digital modulation techniques are used in modern communication systems, such as wireless communication and satellite communication. In these techniques, the modulating signal is a discrete signal, and the modulated carrier signal is also a discrete signal.

###### On-Off Keying (OOK)

In on-off keying, the modulating signal is represented by a series of on and off states. The carrier signal is turned on when the modulating signal is high and turned off when the modulating signal is low. This results in a modulated signal that consists of a series of pulses.

###### Amplitude-Shift Keying (ASK)

In amplitude-shift keying, the modulating signal is represented by different amplitudes of the carrier signal. Each amplitude represents a different symbol or bit. This results in a modulated signal that consists of a series of amplitude-shifted pulses.

###### Frequency-Shift Keying (FSK)

In frequency-shift keying, the modulating signal is represented by different frequencies of the carrier signal. Each frequency represents a different symbol or bit. This results in a modulated signal that consists of a series of frequency-shifted pulses.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the principles of these modulation techniques and discuss their advantages and disadvantages.




#### 7.4c Modulation in Digital Communication

Modulation is a fundamental concept in digital communication, and it plays a crucial role in the efficient transmission of information. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of modulation in digital communication, focusing on the different types of modulation techniques and their applications.

##### Types of Modulation Techniques

There are several types of modulation techniques used in digital communication, each with its own unique characteristics and applications. These include:

- Amplitude Modulation (AM): In AM, the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied to represent the information. This is achieved by multiplying the carrier signal with the modulating signal. The resulting signal is then transmitted over the channel.

- Frequency Modulation (FM): In FM, the frequency of the carrier signal is varied to represent the information. This is achieved by varying the instantaneous frequency of the carrier signal with the modulating signal. The resulting signal is then transmitted over the channel.

- Phase Modulation (PM): In PM, the phase of the carrier signal is varied to represent the information. This is achieved by varying the phase of the carrier signal with the modulating signal. The resulting signal is then transmitted over the channel.

- Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM): In QAM, both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied to represent the information. This is achieved by varying the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal with the modulating signal. The resulting signal is then transmitted over the channel.

##### Applications of Modulation in Digital Communication

Modulation techniques are used in a variety of applications in digital communication. These include:

- Wireless Communication: Modulation techniques are used in wireless communication systems to transmit information over the air. The choice of modulation technique depends on the specific requirements of the system, such as bandwidth, power, and interference.

- Satellite Communication: Modulation techniques are used in satellite communication systems to transmit information between the satellite and the ground station. The choice of modulation technique depends on the specific characteristics of the satellite channel, such as bandwidth, power, and noise.

- Optical Communication: Modulation techniques are used in optical communication systems to transmit information over optical fibers. The choice of modulation technique depends on the specific requirements of the system, such as bandwidth, power, and noise.

- Digital Broadcasting: Modulation techniques are used in digital broadcasting systems to transmit multiple channels of digital data over a single frequency band. The choice of modulation technique depends on the specific requirements of the system, such as bandwidth, power, and interference.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of modulation in digital communication, focusing on the different types of modulation techniques and their applications.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of signal space and modulation, two fundamental principles in digital communication. We have learned that signal space is a mathematical representation of the possible values that a signal can take on, and that modulation is the process of mapping a signal from one signal space to another.

We have also delved into the different types of modulation, including amplitude modulation, frequency modulation, and phase modulation. Each of these modulation techniques has its own unique characteristics and applications, and understanding them is crucial for effective communication.

Furthermore, we have discussed the concept of constellation diagrams, which provide a visual representation of the signal space for different modulation schemes. These diagrams are a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of modulated signals and can aid in the design of communication systems.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive overview of signal space and modulation, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools necessary to understand and apply these principles in digital communication.

Stationarity allows us to make long-term predictions about the behavior of a random process, which is essential in digital communication systems. We will also cover the concept of ergodicity, which is closely related to stationarity and is crucial in the analysis of random processes.

Furthermore, we will explore the concept of random vectors and random matrices, which are essential in the analysis of multiple-input multiple-output (MIMO) systems. We will also discuss the properties of these random variables, such as joint probability density functions and covariance matrices.
